[20] 2003 Passive Hardware Only analyze the packet payload paradigm and uses sketches of measurement structures. Sketches are scalable but provide an approximate measure-ment. In the control plane a standard library of the prototype is used and the data plane is divided into three steps: hashing, classification and counting. The results of the prototype were validated by comparing the prototype with NetFlow. Dharmapurikar et al. [20] implemented bloom filters in hardware for detection predefined signatures (string of bytes) in the packet payload. Signatures were grouped according to their length in bytes and stored in parallel bloom filters. Each stringwas verified if is membership in the bloomfilters. If a string belongs to a bloom filter, this string is declared as a possible matching signature and send to an analyzer. The analyzer is a deterministic algorithm that determines if the string is a member of the set or a false positive. When occurring the match of strings an action (drop, forward, log) is taken over the packet. This work does not use bloom filters to monitor networking performance, it only tracks packets to analyze the packet payload. Narayana et al. [31] proposed a query language (Marple) intended to build networking hardware monitoring switches. Modeled on functional programming constructs, Marple queries are compiled into network programmable switches backed by key-value dictionaries in SRAM and DRAM to store flexible aggregation data per flow. A compiler was implemented to synthesize Marple queries into P4 pro-grammable software and Banzai simulator programmable switch. The experiments ran on two Mininet case studies, with 8-core servers, switch pipelinesRMTwith 32 stages and 224 parallel instructions per stage. The paper shows results for 2 case-studies evaluating UDP and TCP artificial traffic loads, including microbursts and varying flow size distribu-tions, showing that Marple is accurate and provides short query times to avoid invalid keys among eviction tasks. 2.2 Software approaches Narayan et al. [30] proposed a congestion control scheme (CCP) that decouples the datapath from a monolithic ker-nel to a segregated module, which can be implemented in the kernel or in hardware. The module runs together with an agent that contains the API’s definition for the design of novel congestion control algorithms, enabling its reuse across CCP-compliant networking stacks. The API has a common set of primitives, which most congestion control algorithms are based upon and that can be efficiently implemented in the datapath. The datapath has a batchingmechanism to commu-nicate information between the datapath and the agent just 123 R. D. G. Pacífico et al. few times per RTT to overcome the unacceptable overhead of congestion control schemes that, by default, process every incoming ACK. CCP has better mean throughput with the segmentation flag disabled than vanilla Linux. The conges-tion window showed similar behavior for CCP compared to Linux, with slight advantage for Linux in link utilization and median RTT. The CCP prototype has been implemented in Go and the associated Linux Kernel datapath in C. It was tested in links of 1 Gbps and 10 Gbps, with RTT of 10 ms and 1 BDP of buffer. The batching method suffers with low RTT flows (1-10 us). Khandelwal et al.[27] present a stack denominated Con-fluo. It is a software that can be integrated with network management tools for monitoring and diagnosis of in-network events using distributed telemetry data on end-hosts. Moreover, Confluo uses an Atomic MultiLog structure that supports read and write operations of the information about monitored traffic during runtime. To validate the system, the throughput and latency of TCPpacketsweremonitored. Con-fluo was executed on servers with 12-core 2.3 GHz Xeon CPUs and 252 GB RAM. Van Adrichem et al. [47] proposed the OpenNetMon soft-ware.OpenNetMon is an open source application. Itmonitors metrics by flow, in particular, packets delay and loss in network OpenFlow. This application provides traffic engi-neering to the controller with monitoring measurement in real time. The packets loss was calculated based on the dif-ference between the statistics collected from the first and last switch of the network. The packets delay was calculated using the active RTT (Round Trip Time) method. In the tests a video stream was used between two servers in the network border connected to switches with packets delay and loss established by tcpstat software. Gangam et al. [25] developed a technique of passive measurement called ALE (Approximate Latency Estimator). ALE is a technique to estimate the latency based on RTT of the packets. ALE reduces memory consumption to store the state and time associated with TCP/IP packets. This reduc-tion can cause comparison errors between data packets and acknowledge packets. These errors can be solved increasing the memory size based on memory available of the routers. Moshref et al. [29] present different types of measure-ment primitives. The paper evaluates the performance of flow counters, hash and program into CPU to detect Hierarchical Heavy Hitters. For this evaluation various time scales and different setups were used. Dharmapurikar et al. [21] implemented bloom filters for Longest Prefix Matching (LPM). They used bloom filters as an efficient data structure for membership queries in order to determine address prefix membership. Their work was not designed for collecting measurements. Bonomi et. al [7] proposed to track the state of a large num-ber of simultaneous flows using Approximate Concurrent StateMachine (ACSM)over bloomfilters to provideQoSand implement Active Queue Management (AQM) techniques. The authors presented three types of variants of bloom filter: (i) direct bloom filter; (ii) timing-based deletion; (iii) stateful bloom filter. Both variants were used to obtain the current state machine. This work is just theoretical and simulated, it doesn’t apply in the real environment due to limitations of the theoretical models. 2.3 Hybrid approaches Tang et al. [46] developed a heavy flow detection monitor called MV-Sketch. It supports invertible sketch measure-ment primitives to recover information about heavy flows. Invertible sketches are similar to sketches but support the retrieval of all heavy flows. MV-Sketch stores packet flow metadata using a two-dimensional sketch data structure sim-ilar to our bloom filter approach, and is able to detect heavy flows using majority voting, which updates the candidate heavy flow counters in the appropriate sketch based on the current decision of the algorithm. MV-Sketch can have more than one detector with a distributed architecture for moni-toring multiple packet sources. This architecture requires a centralized controller for which the sketches are sent from each detector. At the end of each epoch (measurements are performed in epochs), the controller aggregates the estimates of eachflow, fromwhich it identifies heavyflows.MV-Sketch was implemented and validated in software and on Barefoot Tofino hardware using CAIDA traces. MV-Scketch’s design only supports heavy flow measurement, it does not monitor other network performance or behavior. Gupta et al. [26] created the telemetry system called Sonata. Sonata is a measurement tool based on monitor-ing of the queries. It uses a stream processor to perform the measurements tasks. Sonata has a query interface to cap-ture the information of the queues using a stream processor. This query interface allows network operators to define the behavior of the flows in the queues across the stream pro-cessor. Sonata was implemented over two targets, Barefoot Tofino (hardware with programmable dataplane) and BMV2 (protocol-independent switches in software). For the exper-iment, the chosen metric is heavy hitter detection. Sonata requires more operations with the growth of the number of packets andqueue size. This limitationworsensmeasurement prototype performance. Sonchack et al. [42] present Turboflow, a mixed com-modity switch architecture composed of PFE1 and CPUs for line-rate and fine-grained generation of flow records (FR). PFE manages microflow records (mFR) in an SRAM hash table. To avoidCPUprocessingbottlenecks, Turboflowevicts mFRs to CPU using DMAonly when collisions occur at PFE 1 Programmable Forwarding Engine. 123 BloomTime: space-efficient stateful tracking of time-dependent network performance… SRAM. The Turboflow testbed was composed of 1 ASIC with Tofino PFE, an Intel Quad Core CPU, and an NPU server. Mixed traces of 10 Gb/s collected from Internet core routers and data centers switches were combined to simulate at Tb/s flow rates. Turboflow presented better results with data center traces due to the reduced number of active flows and flow arrival rates. Also, mFR’s collision rate in SRAM and CPU memory occupation are inversely proportional to SRAMallocation. Although PFEwill evolve to increase flex-ibility and reduced costs, hardware solutions still present challenging restrictions, e.g., decomposing complex pro-cessing units in different switches to overcome in-memory concurrency needed by complex packet processing applica-tions. Currently, the costs are restrictive for large adoption. 2.4 Limitations to compare BLOOMTIME with related work Our related work are, indeed, not directly comparable with BloomTime. Section 2.2 shows software approaches that are not directly comparable with hardware ones, although they provide algorithmic strategies and evaluation methodology for future hardware designs such as BloomTime. Meanwhile, works in Sect. 2.1 are comparable on the point of view of the platform, but there is a lack of algo-rithmic comparability: Narayara et al. [31], e.g., show the Marple language for network switches prototyping in hard-ware, which provide uswith useful insights aboutmonitoring tasks in hardware translated from functional query opera-tions; OpenSketch [48] is an approximative measurement solution similar to ours, but differs from an architectural per-spective, with the former using static sketch data tables and the later using time-variant bloom filters to estimate TCP packet arrival times. Dharmapurikar et al. [21] have a similar approach with bloom filters but did not implement a packet arrival time metric. Similar are the conclusions fromworks in Sect. 2.3, which inspired our methodology and provided algorithmic insights, but a scenario composed with control and data planes can not be deployed to allow a direct comparison. For example, MV-Sketch [46] implements a hybrid solution to extend tradi-tional sketch approaches (e.g., OpenSketch) to allow retrieval of heavy flows using static data structures optimized to such tasks. MV-Sketch differs from BloomTime in its control plane to aggregate traffic information and in the algorithmic approach (from sketch data tables to bloom filters). 3 Bloom filter overview Bloom filters are probabilistic data structures based on set theory [6]. Bloom filters are space-efficient data structures that allow membership queries. [10]. In computer network- ing, bloom filters are widely used in different tasks. For example, caching of Web servers and storage servers [9,13, 22]; processing P2P networks [11,12,36,40]; packet routing and forwarding [19,43]; monitoring and measurement [23, 41,44]. The bloom filter structure is represented by a set S ={ x1, x2, ..., xn } of e stored elements in an array of m bits, zero-filled by default. Moreover, k distinct hash functions h1,...,hk are used to map an item in the array. A value x is said to belong a bloom filter when the array bits indexed hi (x) are equal to 1 for all i , 1 ≤ i ≤ k. The main drawback of bloom filters is related to the false positive rate. False positives occur when the query of an item not inserted in the bloom filter returns that the item belongs in the set. The false positive probability can be calculated according to Eq. 1 [10]. In this equation, e is the number of inserted elements,m is the number of positions of the bloom filter and k represents the number of hash functions. Note that Eq. 1 assumes a hash function that distributes all sets uniformly across the m positions. f p = ( 1 − ( 1 − 1 m )ke )k ≈ ( 1 − e−ke/m )k (1) Equation 2 indicates the optimum number of hashes in the bloom filters. k = m e ln(2) (2) Bloom filters support two types of operations: inser-tion and query. Bloom filters don’t support deletion of an item. Figure 1 presents examples of the insertion and query operations onbloomfilter structure: (A) thebloomfilter is ini-tializedwith zeros; (B) item X is inserted. The hash functions h1 and h2 map the item X to positions 0 and 6, respectively. If these positions were 0, they are updated to 1. (C) Suppose that some items were inserted and there is a query for the item Y . Then, item Y is mapped to positions 2 and 4 by the hash functions. If both positions have value 1, Y is said to be an element of the bloom filter, which may be a false positive if Y was never inserted. 3.1 Variants of bloom filter In this subsection, we describe four variants of bloom filters. Every variant is composed of distinct features to improve the performance of standard bloom filters. 3.1.1 Counting bloom filter Counting bloom filter (CBF) [22] is an improvement of stan-dard bloom filters to allow deletion operation. This structure 123 R. D. G. Pacífico et al. Fig. 1 Bloom filter operations example (a) (b) (c) uses an array of counters instead of an array of bits. The counters are composed of four or more bits. The arithmetic overflow is solved establishing a value sufficiently large for the counters (for example, 4 bits). An item is inserted in this structure incrementing the k counters of the item instead of marking its k positions. The query operation is similar to the standard bloom filter. In this operation, the counters of the item are verified if they aren’t equal to zero. In the deletion operation, an item is removed decrementing the value of the k counters. CBF consumes 4x more space than a standard bloom filter due to the size of the counters. A solution to this problem is to add a hash table to manage the overflowing counters. However, it generates an additional complexity to manage the structure. 3.1.2 D-left counting bloom filter D-left counting bloom filter (d-Left CBF) [8] is an extension of CBF. This structure appears as a big array. It uses a hash table composed of buckets with a fixed number of cells. Each cell stores a fingerprint and a counter. A fingerprint is formed by twoparts: bucket index (it stores the hash value of the item) and the remainder part of the fingerprint. An item is stored in this structure incrementing the counters and storing the respective fingerprint. The query and deletion are equal to CBF. D-left CBF compared to CBF reduces the space cost by 50%. However, it consumes 1.5 - 2x more space than standard bloom filters. 3.1.3 Blocked bloom filter Blocked bloom filter (BBF) [37] is a structure composed of a set of small bloom filters stored in the CPU cache. Each CPU cache line represents a small bloom filter. In this structure, an item is stored in one of these small bloom filter according to the result of a hash function. BBF doesn’t support dele-tion operation. However, it provides better spatial locality on query operation. Each query operation causes one cache miss to load the bloom filter. This significantly improves the performance of BBF. The main drawback of this structure is the high false positive rate due to the imbalanced load caused by the array of small bloom filters. 3.1.4 Quotient filter Quotient filter (QF) [4] is a structure that uses a compact hash table F with m = 2q buckets called quotienting to sup-port deletion operation using fingerprints. Fingerprints are divided into two parts r and q, where r stores the least sig-nificant bits of the fingerprint andq stores themost significant bits of the quotient (q = p − r ). The parameter p is the bit number of a generated hash value. The insertion, query, and deletion operations can be performed by storing, searching, and removing the hash value in F .The main advantage of QF is to expand and shrink with elements insertion or removal. However, if QF is compared to a standard bloom filter, it consumes 10 to 25% more area. 4 BLOOMTIME architecture BloomTime is a network measurement primitive that uses bloomfilters to track time-dependentmetrics of network traf-fic at line rate. BloomTime operates passively and adds no traffic to the network. In BloomTime, time is divided into n fixed windows of w milliseconds. BloomTime maintains one bloom filter Bi for each time window. Bloom filter B0 contains information about the current time window and Bn contains information about the oldest timewindowstill being tracked. Periodically, at every w milliseconds, the time windows are updated. The values in bucket Bi are moved to bucket Bi+1 and B0 is reset. We define the current time as tnow, and the time of the last update as T . Bucket B0 contains information about packets that traversed BloomTime within [tnow, T ) and bucket Bi stores information about packets that traversed BloomTime within [T + iw, T + (i + 1)w). BloomTime keeps infor-mation about n windows of duration w, so the total history over which time-dependent metrics can be computed covers a duration W = nw. After W milliseconds, the informa-tion (in bucket Bn) is removed from BloomTime due to the periodic update operations of the windows. Figure 2 illustrates the insertion of a packet in Bloom-Time. When BloomTime processes a packet, a key is extracted from the packet. The packet key is used by the 123 BloomTime: space-efficient stateful tracking of time-dependent network performance… Fig. 2 BloomTime architecture hash functions to compute the corresponding bloom filter positions for the packet. Different key definitions allow the definition of different flow aggregates. For example, per-flow monitoring can be performed by defining each packet’s key to be the usual 5-tuple flow definition,2 and traffic for IP des-tination prefixes of size k can be monitored by defining each packet’s key as the k most-significant bits of each packet’s destination IP address. BloomTime supports any function that extracts a sequence of bytes from the packet as its key. When each packet arrives, the positions given by the hash functions are marked on filter B0. After packets were pro-cessed, they are forwarded by BloomTime. BloomTime is agnostic to traffic aggregates,which can be cleverlymanipulated to capture diversemetrics. For example, BloomTime can measure RTTs by defining aggregates that include an (outgoing) packet and the expected (incoming) response.3 In particular, BloomTime can passively measure RTT between its hosting device and arbitrary destinations piggybacking on existing ping measurements. For exam-ple, the expected response to an outgoing ping (ICMP Echo Request) packet simply exchanges the source and destina-tion IP addresses and changes the ICMP code in the packet header to an ICMP Echo Reply, allowing the definition of an aggregate that matches both ping and response.4 A similar idea applies to existing TCP connections [25]. We can use the content stored in the bloom filters to estimate time-dependent metrics. Estimation accuracy is a function of the time window durationw, and traffic coverage is a function of the total window duration W . Accuracy and 2 A 5-tuple flow definition computes hash functions over the (source address, destination address, source port, destination port, and protocol identifier) IP header fields. 3 We assume that both forward and reverse paths traverseBloomTime. 4 The hash function for outgoing packets exchanges position of IP addresses and ICMP code, while the hash function for incoming packets does not. Fig. 3 BloomTime implementation with support to packet interarrival time measurement coverage are related by the total amount of SRAM available to store bloom filters. 5 BLOOMTIME implementation Figure 3 presents an overview of the BloomTime’s imple-mentation in theNetFPGA [33] platform. TheBloomTime’s implementation is composed of two main components: the datapath and userspace tools. The datapath component contains hardware modules that belong to the standard NetF-PGA library (dark gray modules) and specific modules to BloomTime (light gray modules). The userspace compo-nent contains a measurement software that is responsible for reading the markings of the bloom filters and for computing the desired metrics. In this work, we demonstrate userspace tools to compute the packet inter-arrival time variance and mean for individual flows. The NetFPGA standard library already provides the mod-ules: input arbiter, output port lookup and output queue. The input arbiter receives packets from the MAC queues or CPU queues, and sends the packets to the output port lookupmod-ule. The output port lookup computes on which port the packet should be sent based on packet properties (e.g., an input port or destination address), and sets the output port metadata field for all packets. Finally, the output queue stores and transmits the packets in the MAC or CPU output queues according to the output port lookup module definition. BloomTime extends the NetFPGA’s pipeline with five hardware modules (light gray): time counter, header parser, hash functions, bloom filter controller and SRAM backend. Time counter works on clock cycles. Header parser extracts header fields that compose the packet keys. Hash functions are generated to index the bloom filters. Bloom filter con-troller is responsible for setting indexed bits in the B0 bucket, moves the data of Bi to Bi+1 and resets B0 everyw millisec- 123 R. D. G. Pacífico et al. Fig. 4 BloomTime datapath implemented in the NetFPGA hardware onds. SRAM backend controls the access to SRAMmemory, where the bloom filters are stored. We detail these modules in Sect. 5.1. 5.1 Datapath We implement standard bloom filters on the NetFPGA’s platform because standard bloom filters are synthesizable in hardware without additional cost (e.g., counters, hash table and fingerprint). Variants of bloom filter cannot be synthesizable due to the additional cost to improve the limita-tions of standard bloom filters. Figure 4 shows an overview of BloomTime’s datapath. The input arbiter (not shown) receives packets from MAC or CPU queues, serialized them into a single stream, and forwards them to the header parser module. The header parser module first checks whether the packet belongs to a TCP flow and, if so, extracts the source and des-tination addresses and ports, forwarding the flow identifier to the hash module. If the packet does not belong to a TCP flow, it is ignored by all the BloomTime modules. When the hashmodule receives the flow identifier, it generates two indexes using hash functions h1 and h2 to store the packet in the bloom filters. The hash functions generate values within [0,m − 1]. The hashmodule sends the two indexes to the bloom filter controller. The bloom filter controller loads from the SRAM lines corresponding to h1 and h2, sets bit B0 on both lines, and writes them back to the SRAM.5 The bloom filter controller uses a FIFO queue to track which indexes need to have the B0 bit set for cases where there is memory contention. The time counter is a software register that counts clock cycles and sends a signal to thebloomfilter controller everyw milliseconds to trigger the update of the bloom filter buckets. The bloom filter controller module starts to move the buckets when the bloom filter controller is not performing any memory operations (i.e., when it has an empty queue). 5.2 Userspace Our prototype also has userspace tools to collect the con-tents of the bloom filters from the NetFPGA’s memory and compute the mean and variance of packet inter-arrival times for each flow. In more detail, the measurement software per-forms three functions: (i) observes the hardware registers; (ii) reads bloom filters marks; (iii) initializes SRAM values at the end of a reading process. 5 The NetFPGA SRAM is addressable only per line. When marking just a bit corresponding to B0, our prototype needs to read the entire line and write it back. Other architectures might allow for write-only implementations. 123 BloomTime: space-efficient stateful tracking of time-dependent network performance… The measurement software reads monitoring time mark-ing of the bloom filters inside to SRAM. Recall that, once a monitoring time expires, the system enables hardware regis-ter. Measurement software then can read flows arrival time bloomfiltersmarks, through the registers interface. Themea-surement software saves data in a local file, to be further processed by end-users. After this process, bloom filters measurement modules are initialized. The bloom filters are initialized automatically over time due to displacements. Each monitoring time period has a duration equal to the product of the number of bloom filters and the time window size. At the end of each period, a hardware register is acti-vated, and the measurement software executes the reading of markings via the registers interface. Then, the software performs mean and variance flow packet inter-arrival calcu-lation, based on bloom filters marks. Measurement software can calculate flow mean inter-arrival packet time to observe the time difference between two consecutive bloom filters, containing the same hash sig-natures. Note that, both hashes must be marked in a bloom filter. In case of at least one of them is not marked, bloom filter doesn’t contain information about the observed flow. As a consequence, flow inter-arrival time isn’t computed. 5.3 Hardware instantiation In this work, we use the NetFPGA 1G, which contains 4 Kb of SRAM and an FPGA Xilinx Virtex-II Pro 50. Each memory line is 72-bit long. We use 8 bits as parity bits and use each remaining bit as a bloom filter (n = 64). We store bloom filter positions on different lines and use 8,192 lines of SRAM for all bloom filters (k = 8, 192). Based on Eq. 2 and considering an expected population of 2000 simultaneous flows, we use two hash functions to compute packet indexes in the bloom filters. In the current work, we use different time window sizes w, varying between 8 and 23 milliseconds, to evaluate accuracy as a function of window sizes. There are more powerful NetFPGA models than the first-generation 1G model. For example, the NetFPGA SUME [45] (FPGA Virtex-7) has 13x more logic cells than the 1Gmodel. More memory would allowmore (increasem) and larger bloom filters (increase k). Faster FPGAs would allow the implementation of more complex packet keys and hash functions. 6 Experimental environment and evaluation methodology In this section, we describe the testbed created to evaluate the BloomTime. Our goal is to validate the implementation of the BloomTime comparing the accuracy of measurements Fig. 5 Testbed environment between hardware and software. Moreover, we compare both, exact and approximated measurement approaches. 6.1 Testbed environment Figure 5 shows the realistic scenario configured to evaluate the BloomTime. This scenario was based on our previous work [34,35] to allow a fair comparison between the exact and approximated measurements approaches. In short, we generate controlled traffic from host Ai (1 ≤ i ≤ 20) to host B. Hosts A1 to A20 generate up to 2000 flows simultaneously in thenetwork.Duringour experiments, hosts A1 to A20 generate up to 2000 flows simultaneously in the network, sending rawTCPpackets according to the TCPflow or congestion control. The two traditional switcheswere added to generate a real-istic delay between node Ai and B. The host B is connected to a switch running the BloomTime. Host B is running the tcpdump6 tool. The traffic measured by tcpdump is com-pared with the measurements of the BloomTime. 6.2 Evaluationmetrics We evaluate BloomTime predicting mean packet inter-arrival times and their variance for each flow Tk for each flow k. At each end of W window, the SRAM content is moved via DMA to the host, where we compute the mean and variance of packet inter-arrival times. The inter-arrivel time between consecutive packets i and j of a flow is esti-mated by (wj+1 −wj)/2− (wi+1 −wi)/2, wherewi denotes the beginning of the window when packet i was observed by BloomTime. The mean inter-arrival time for flow k can be estimated by 1/(Nk − 1) ∑Nk n=1 Tnk , where nk identifies a pair of consecutive packets of flow k and Nk is the number of packets observed for flow k. Finally, the variance can be estimated by the formula: ∑Nk n=1 T 2 nk (Nk−1) − ( ∑Nk n=1 Tnk (Nk−1) ) 2. 6 Available in: http://www.tcpdump.org/. 123 R. D. G. Pacífico et al. 6.3 Evaluationmethodology Each host Ai runs up to 100 threads to generate flows to BloomTime validation. The generated flows were moni-tored by BloomTime during the experiments. We installed measurement rules in the NetFPGA’s TCAM before start the experiment to avoid control plane bias. We generate rawTCPpackets before each execution of the experiment using Libnet library7. The packets generated contains a header composed by the payload, source IP, des-tination IP, source port and destination port. The experiment starts when each host Ai sends pre-generated TCP packets to host B using the bittwist8 traffic generator. The traffic generated was predefined with inter-packet time (20 and 40 ms). Moreover, we also evaluated random inter-packet time from 0 to 50 ms following a uniform dis-tribution. We have varied the number of flow from 28, 1000 and 2000 flows. We use 8 and 23 ms as the value of the time window of the BloomTime. Each experiment was executed 50 times. The experiments with time window 8 ms have had a duration of 33.6 s. The experiments with time window 23 ms have had a duration of 81.75 s (1 minute and 36 s). We removed the measurements of the three first and last execu-tions for the experiments with time window 8 ms and the eight first and last executions for the experiments with time window 23 ms due to the warm up time of the experiment. We considered 10% of the duration time of the experiments to define the number of removed samples. We capture in each host Ai all traffic sent to host B. We also use the tcpdump to perform this task. The tcpdump was executed in all the hosts. Pcap files were created in each host during the tcpdump execution. The elapsed time of each pcap file was equal to the total time of the experiment plus the time to initiale and finish the experiment. We repro-duce these files using the DPKT9 library in Python language to compare BloomTime with the measurement approach in software. The markings of the bloom filters were read at the end of each iteration of the experiment. After the BloomTime’s modules are reset, all hosts finalize sending packets. 7 Results In this section, we present the packet interarrival time results using two benchmarks (predefined and random delay). In the first benchmark, packets were sent with alternating intervals of 20 and 40 ms. In the second benchmark, packets were transmitted with random intervals from 0 to 50 ms following 7 Available in: https://sourceforge.net/projects/libnet/. 8 Available in: http://bittwist.sourceforge.net. 9 Available in: https://dpkt.readthedocs.io/. a uniform distribution which is closer to realistic traffic [15]. We executed each benchmark with 28, 1000 and 2000 flows and time windows of 8 and 23 ms. We defined the values of the predefined and random delay based on [34] to reproduce the results. The goal of the experiment with 28 flows is to verify the behavior of theBloomTime for a small number of flows.We chose 28 flows to compare the BloomTime with the exact approach. In the experiment with 1000 and 2000 flows, we verify if the BloomTime scales. In both experiments, we observed the impact of the false positives rate based on the increase in the number of flows. Moreover, the results were presented using the BoxPlot graphic with a confidence interval. The BoxPlot graphic rep-resents the population distribution divided into parts called quartiles. It is composed of four quartiles. The first quar-tile (Q1) refers to 25%, the second quartile (median) has a percentile of 50%, and the third quartile (Q3) is equivalent to 75% of the samples. Q1 is represented in the graphic as the lower bound of the box, the median refers to the middle line and the high bound of the box represents Q3. The traces in the edges after the box are called whiskers. They repre-sent the lower and upper bounds of the samples. We used this type of graphic to represent the distribution of the rela-tive and absolute difference between hardware and software measurements. 7.1 Fixed delay bechmark 7.1.1 Experiment with 28 flows In the mean relative difference graphic (Fig. 6b), 75% of the measurements are between 0 and 5% for the time windows 8 and 23 ms. The value of mean and standard deviation for this time windows is low, 2.5% and 1.8% the mean and 2.0% and 1.62% the standard deviation. The values expected for mean are into of the confidence interval [2.38:2.62]% for 8 ms and [1.73:1.92]% for 23 ms. In the absolute difference graphic (Fig. 6a), the samples are between 0 and 10 ms for respective windows. In the variance graphic (Fig. 7b), 75% of the samples have a relative difference between hardware and software of 0 to 30%. The variance measured by hardware has a higher value in relation to software due to the operations of moving the content of the bloom filters. These operations cause more variation in the accuracy of the measurements. Figure 7a shows the absolute difference in the variance. The values of the absolute difference vary between 0 and 20 µs for the respective time windows. Table 14 Best time windows - fixed delay Flows Mean (ms) Variance (ms) 28 23 23 1000 23 23 2000 23 08 7.5 BLOOMTIME limitations TheBloomTimemeasures up to 2000flows providing a scal-able solution sacrificing the measurements’ accuracy. This occurs due to three factors: update the bloom filters, size of the time window and occurrence of false positives (colli-sions). The BloomTime is composed of 64 bloom filters with 8192 lines. The bloom filters are stored in the SRAM. Fig-ure 4 shows the structure of the bloom filters in the SRAM. When the time window has reached the operations to update the time windows are enabled. The update process is com- 123 R. D. G. Pacífico et al. (a) (b) Fig. 20 Mean graphics: best results for random delay (a) (b) Fig. 21 Variance graphics: best results for random delay Table 15 Best time windows - random delay Flows Mean (ms) Variance (ms) 28 08 23 1000 08 23 2000 08 08 posed of reading and writing operations in the SRAM. This process is repeated 8192 times. The elapsed time is equal to 400 µs of the time window, and it affects the measurement accuracy in a small time scale (ms). The size of the timewindow is another critical factor of the architecture. The markings of the flows interarrival time are performed based on the size of the time window. Large time windows harm measurement accuracy. Small time windows, for example, 2ms, increase themeasurement accuracy. How-ever, to improve the accuracy harm variance measurements due to the increase in the number of updates. The BloomTime uses two hash functions to address the flows in the bloom filters. Different flows can address the same positions causing the emergence of false positives (col-lisions).When themeasurement software reads the markings of the SRAM and finds the false positives in a flow, the measurement of the flow is discarded. The discarded mea-surement harms the measurement’s accuracy. The graphic (Fig. 25) presents the directly proportional relationship of the increase in the number of flows and false positives. This graphic wasmade using Eq. 1 and the valuesm, e and k of the BloomTime, m = 8192, e = 2000 and k = 2 were entered. For 2000 flows the occurrence rate of false positives in the BloomTime is 14.92%. Exact monitoring approaches measure accurately. The factors described above don’t affect the measurements of these types of approaches. These approaches use flow coun-ters that consume CPU resources. Moreover, they measure a small number of flows due to the size of the memory avail-able. The memory of these approaches is small and limited. Approximatemonitoring approachesmeasure a large volume of flows approximately sacrificing the accuracy by scalabil-ity. 123 BloomTime: space-efficient stateful tracking of time-dependent network performance… (a) (b) Fig. 22 Exact x approximate graphics: fixed delay (a) (b) Fig. 23 Exact x approximate graphics: random delay Fig. 24 Tendency lines graphic of the approximate measurement of the prototype Fig. 25 Number of flows x false positives 123 R. D. G. Pacífico et al. 8 Conclusion Traffic monitoring tasks are essential for modern traffic engi-neering. Unfortunately, to measure a large volume of traffic in real time accurately become challenging using traditional methods in IP networks. In this paper, we proposed and implemented the architecture in hardware called Bloom-Time. BloomTimemeasures the packets interarrival time of way approximated. We evaluated BloomTime in a realistic environment. We also compared the approximate measure-ment of the prototype with an exact monitoring prototype. Our goal was to present the advantages and drawbacks of both approaches. BloomTime shows thatwe canmeasure results up to 2000 flows with a rate of 14.92% of false positives. However, it performs a less accurate measurement in comparison to the exact prototype because the BloomTime sacrifice the accu-racy for scalability. As future work, we intend to implement bloom filters out-side of the SRAM using registers. We will implement other types of variants of bloom filters over the NetFPGA’s plat-form. We will also implement other metric types available in the literature. Moreover, we will compare the performance between variants of bloom filter with different metrics over NetFPGA’s platform.
